        <td>
         Channel 23
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <th scope="row">
         <a href="/wiki/Eir_(telecommunications)" title="Eir (telecommunications)">
          Eir Vision
         </a>
        </th>
        <td>
         Channel 201
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <th colspan="2" style="text-align:center;background-color: #d0e5f5;">
         <a href="/wiki/Streaming_media" title="Streaming media">
          Streaming media
         </a>
        </th>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <th scope="row">
         <a href="/wiki/BBC_iPlayer" title="BBC iPlayer">
          BBC iPlayer
         </a>
        </th>
        <td>
         <a class="external text" href="https://www.bbc.co.uk/iplayer/live/bbcone" rel="nofollow">
          Watch live
         </a>
         (UK only)
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <th scope="row">
         TVPlayer
        </th>
        <td>
         <a class="external text" href="http://tvplayer.com/watch/bbcone" rel="nofollow">
          Watch Live
         </a>
         (UK only)
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <th scope="row">
         Horizon Go
        </th>
        <td>
         <a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20130921054646/https://www.horizon.tv/en_ie/live-channels/live-channel.html/28867111108/BBC-1.html" rel="nofollow">
          Watch live
         </a>
         (Ireland only)
         <br/>
         <a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20130921053311/https://www.horizon.tv/de_ch/live-sender/live-channel.html/48325671444/BBC-One.html" rel="nofollow">
          Watch live
         </a>
         (Switzerland only)
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <th scope="row">
         Ziggo GO
        </th>
        <td>
         <a class="external text" href="https://www.ziggogo.tv" rel="nofollow">
          ZiggoGO.tv
         </a>
         (Netherlands only)
        </td>
       </tr>
      </table>
      <p>
       <b>
        BBC One
       </b>
       is the flagship
       <a href="/wiki/Television_channel" title="Television channel">
        television channel
       </a>
       of the
       <a href="/wiki/BBC" title="BBC">
        British Broadcasting Corporation
       </a>
       in the
       <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">
        United Kingdom
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Isle_of_Man" title="Isle of Man">
        Isle of Man
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Channel_Islands" title="Channel Islands">
        Channel Islands
       </a>
       . It was launched on 2 November 1936 as the
       <b>
        BBC Television Service
       </b>
       , and was the world's first regular television service with a high level of
       <a href="/wiki/Image_resolution" title="Image resolution">
        image resolution
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-2">
         [2]
        </a>
       </sup>
       It was renamed
       <b>
        BBC TV
       </b>
       in 1960, using this name until the launch of sister channel
       <a href="/wiki/BBC_Two" title="BBC Two">
        BBC2
       </a>
       in 1964, whereupon the BBC TV channel became known as
       <b>
        BBC1
       </b>
       , with the current spelling adopted in 1997.
      </p>
      <p>
       The channel's annual budget for 2012–13 is £1.14 billion.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Service_licence_3-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Service_licence-3">
         [3]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The channel is funded by the
       <a href="/wiki/Television_licence" title="Television licence">
        television licence
       </a>
       fee together with the BBC's other domestic
       <a href="/wiki/Television_station" title="Television station">
        television stations
       </a>
       , and therefore shows uninterrupted programming without commercial advertising. It is currently the most watched television channel in the United Kingdom, ahead of its traditional rival for ratings leadership,
       <a href="/wiki/ITV_(TV_network)" title="ITV (TV network)">
        ITV
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       As of June 2013
       <sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;">
        <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=BBC_One&amp;action=edit">
         [update]
        </a>
       </sup>
       the channel controller for BBC One is
       <a href="/wiki/Charlotte_Moore_(TV_executive)" title="Charlotte Moore (TV executive)">
        Charlotte Moore
       </a>
       , who succeeded
       <a href="/wiki/Danny_Cohen_(television_executive)" title="Danny Cohen (television executive)">
        Danny Cohen
       </a>
       initially as an Acting Controller from May 2013.
      </p>
      <p>
      </p>

      <p>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="History">
        History
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=BBC_One&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Early_years_and_launching">
        Early years and launching
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=BBC_One&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Early years and launching">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Dummy_emitron_camera.JPG">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1035" data-file-width="472" height="373" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Dummy_emitron_camera.JPG/170px-Dummy_emitron_camera.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Dummy_emitron_camera.JPG/255px-Dummy_emitron_camera.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Dummy_emitron_camera.JPG/340px-Dummy_emitron_camera.JPG 2x" width="170"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Dummy_emitron_camera.JPG" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Replica of an Emitron camera used to make the earliest 405-line programmes broadcast on the channel
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/BBC_Television" title="BBC Television">
        BBC Television
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       The BBC began its own regular television programming from the basement of
       <a href="/wiki/Broadcasting_House" title="Broadcasting House">
        Broadcasting House
       </a>
       , London, on 22 August 1932. The BBC Television Service officially began regular broadcasts on 2 November 1936 from a converted wing of the
       <a href="/wiki/Alexandra_Palace" title="Alexandra Palace">
        Alexandra Palace
       </a>
       in London.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4">
        <a href="#cite_note-4">
         [4]
        </a>
       </sup>
       On 1 September 1939, two days before Britain declared
       <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">
        war
       </a>
       on Germany, the station was taken off air with little warning, with one of the last programmes to be shown before the suspension of the service being a
       <a href="/wiki/Mickey_Mouse" title="Mickey Mouse">
        Mickey Mouse
       </a>
       cartoon;
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MickeyMouse_5-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-MickeyMouse-5">
         [5]
        </a>
       </sup>
       the government was concerned that the VHF transmissions would act as a beacon to enemy aircraft homing in on London. BBC Television returned on 7 June 1946 at 15:00.
       <a href="/wiki/Jasmine_Bligh" title="Jasmine Bligh">
        Jasmine Bligh
       </a>
       , one of the original announcers, made the first announcement, saying, "Good afternoon everybody. How are you? Do you remember me, Jasmine Bligh?". The Mickey Mouse cartoon of 1939 was repeated twenty minutes later.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6">
        <a href="#cite_note-6">
         [6]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Creation_of_BBC_One">
        Creation of BBC One
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=BBC_One&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Creation of BBC One">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       The BBC held a statutory
       <a href="/wiki/Monopoly" title="Monopoly">
        monopoly
       </a>
       on television broadcasting in the United Kingdom until the first
       <a href="/wiki/Associated-Rediffusion" title="Associated-Rediffusion">
        Independent Television station
       </a>
       began to broadcast on 22 September 1955, when
       <a href="/wiki/ITV_(TV_network)" title="ITV (TV network)">
        ITV
       </a>
       started broadcasting. The competition quickly forced the channel to change its identity and priorities following a large reduction in its audience.
      </p>
      <p>
       The 1962
       <a href="/wiki/Pilkington_Committee_on_Broadcasting" title="Pilkington Committee on Broadcasting">
        Pilkington Report
       </a>
       on the future of broadcasting noticed this, and that ITV lacked any serious programming. It therefore decided that Britain's third television station should be awarded to the BBC.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7">
        <a href="#cite_note-7">
         [7]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The station, renamed BBC TV in 1960, became BBC1 when
       <a href="/wiki/BBC_Two" title="BBC Two">
        BBC2
       </a>
       was launched on 20 April 1964 transmitting an incompatible
       <a href="/wiki/576i" title="576i">
        625-line image
       </a>
       on UHF. The only way to receive all channels was to use a complex "dual-standard" 405- and 625-line, VHF and UHF, receiver, with both a VHF and a UHF
       <a href="/wiki/Television_antenna" title="Television antenna">
        aerial
       </a>
       . Old 405-line-only sets became obsolete in 1985, when transmission in the standard ended, although standards converters have become available for enthusiasts who collect and restore such TVs.
      </p>
      <p>
       BBC1 was based at the purpose-built
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/BBC_Television_Centre" title="BBC Television Centre">
        BBC Television Centre
       </a>
       at
       <a href="/wiki/White_City,_London" title="White City, London">
        White City, London
       </a>
       between 1960 and 2013.
       <a href="/wiki/BBC_News#1950s" title="BBC News">
        Television News
       </a>
       continued to use Alexandra Palace as its base—by early 1968 it had even converted one of its studios to colour—before moving to new purpose-built facilities at Television Centre on 20 September 1969.
      </p>
      <p>
       In the weeks leading up to 15 November 1969, BBC1 unofficially transmitted the occasional programme in its new colour system, to test it. At midnight on 15 November, simultaneously with
       <a href="/wiki/ITV_(TV_network)" title="ITV (TV network)">
        ITV
       </a>
       and two years after BBC2, BBC1 officially began 625-line
       <a href="/wiki/PAL" title="PAL">
        PAL
       </a>
       colour programming on UHF with a broadcast of a concert by
       <a href="/wiki/Petula_Clark" title="Petula Clark">
        Petula Clark
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8">
        <a href="#cite_note-8">
         [8]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Colour transmissions could be received (in monochrome) on monochrome 625-line sets until the end of analogue broadcasting.
      </p>
      <p>
       In terms of audience share, the most successful period for BBC1 was under
       <a href="/wiki/Bryan_Cowgill" title="Bryan Cowgill">
        Bryan Cowgill
       </a>
       between 1973 and 1977, when the channel achieved an average audience share of 45%.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9">
        <a href="#cite_note-9">
         [9]
        </a>
       </sup>
       This period is still regarded by many as a golden age of the BBC's output, with the BBC achieving a very high standard across its entire range of series, serials, plays, light entertainment and documentaries.
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:No_original_research" title="Wikipedia:No original research">
          <span title="The material near this tag possibly contains original research. (June 2014)">
           original research?
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       On 30 December 1980, the BBC announced their intention to introduce a new
       <a href="/wiki/Breakfast_television" title="Breakfast television">
        breakfast television
       </a>
       service to compete with
       <a href="/wiki/TV-am" title="TV-am">
        TV-am
       </a>
       . The BBC stated it would start broadcasting before TV-am, but made clear their hands were tied until November 1981 when the new licence fee income became available, to help finance extending broadcast hours, with the hope of starting in 1982. On 17 January 1983, the first edition of
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Breakfast_Time" title="Breakfast Time">
         Breakfast Time
        </a>
       </i>
       was shown on BBC One, becoming the first UK wide breakfast television service
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10">
        <a href="#cite_note-10">
         [10]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and continued to lead in the ratings until 1984.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11">
        <a href="#cite_note-11">
         [11]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12">
        <a href="#cite_note-12">
         [12]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span id="Michael_Grade_era_.281984.E2.80.931987.29">
       </span>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Michael_Grade_era_(1984–1987)">
        Michael Grade era (1984–1987)
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=BBC_One&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Michael Grade era (1984–1987)">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       In 1984,
       <a href="/wiki/Bill_Cotton" title="Bill Cotton">
        Bill Cotton
       </a>
       become managing director of Television at the BBC, and set about overhauling BBC1, which had been slated for poor home grown shows, its heavy reliance on US imports, with
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Dallas_(1978_TV_series)" title="Dallas (1978 TV series)">
         Dallas
        </a>
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Thorn_Birds_(TV_miniseries)" title="The Thorn Birds (TV miniseries)">
         The Thorn Birds
        </a>
       </i>
       being BBC1's highest rated programmes and ratings being over 20% behind ITV. Cotton recruited
       <a href="/wiki/Michael_Grade" title="Michael Grade">
        Michael Grade
       </a>
       to become Controller of BBC One, the first time the Corporation had recruited someone outside of the BBC,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13">
        <a href="#cite_note-13">
         [13]
        </a>
       </sup>
       replacing Alan Hart, who has been criticised for his lack of knowledge in general entertainment, as he was head of BBC Sport prior to 1981.
      </p>
      <p>
       The first major overhaul was to axe the deeply unpopular
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Sixty_Minutes_(TV_series)" title="Sixty Minutes (TV series)">
         Sixty Minutes
        </a>
       </i>
       current affairs programme: this was a replacement for the news and magazine show
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Nationwide_(TV_programme)" title="Nationwide (TV programme)">
         Nationwide
        </a>
       </i>
       . Its replacement was the
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/BBC_News_at_Six" title="BBC News at Six">
         BBC Six O'Clock News
        </a>
       </i>
       ,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14">
        <a href="#cite_note-14">
         [14]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15">
        <a href="#cite_note-15">
         [15]
        </a>
       </sup>
       a straight new programme in a bid to shore up its failing early evening slot. It was believed the BBC were planning to cut short the evening news and move more light entertainment programming in from the 18:20 slot, but this was dismissed. The
       <a href="/wiki/Miss_Great_Britain" title="Miss Great Britain">
        Miss Great Britain
       </a>
       contest was dropped, being described as verging on the too offensive after the January 1985 contest, with
       <i>
        Worlds Strongest Man
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        International Superstar
       </i>
       also being axed.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16">
        <a href="#cite_note-16">
         [16]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       BBC1 was relaunched on 18 February 1985 with a
       <a href="/wiki/Computer_Originated_World" title="Computer Originated World">
        new look
       </a>
       , new programming including
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Wogan" title="Wogan">
         Wogan
        </a>
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/EastEnders" title="EastEnders">
         EastEnders
        </a>
       </i>
       and a revised schedule to help streamline and maintain viewers throughout the course of the evening. Grade started to gear most programmes to either on the hour or half past the hour, while
       <i>
        Panorama
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        Omnibus
       </i>
       were both moved after the
       <i>
        Nine O'Clock News
       </i>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17">
        <a href="#cite_note-17">
         [17]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Grade was also determined to end the dated and inept BBC1 scheduling which was hampering the network and which was holding back good programmes. Grade stated "When I took over BBC1, I discovered there were wonderful things, it was just a case of where to put them."
       <i>
        Wogan
       </i>
       had been scheduled for a 10 pm slot, but Grade moved it to a 7 pm slot as he believed the show had potential.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-transdiffusion.org_18-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-transdiffusion.org-18">
         [18]
        </a>
       </sup>
       From February to August 1985, a high amount of American mini series were broadcast while filming took place of a number of new home grown programmes, including
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/%27Allo_%27Allo!" title="'Allo 'Allo!">
         'Allo 'Allo!
        </a>
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/In_Sickness_and_in_Health" title="In Sickness and in Health">
         In Sickness and in Health
        </a>
       </i>
       , and
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Open_All_Hours" title="Open All Hours">
         Open All Hours
        </a>
       </i>
       . Further improvement come about when the corporation strengthened its drama output costing £30 million, with eight new series, including
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Howards%27_Way" title="Howards' Way">
         Howards' Way
        </a>
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/All_Creatures_Great_and_Small_(TV_series)" title="All Creatures Great and Small (TV series)">
         All Creatures Great and Small
        </a>
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        Hold the Back Page
       </i>
       , and
       <i>
        Bluebill
       </i>
       , along with the return of
       <i>
        Bergerac
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        Big Deal
       </i>
       . The increase in the drama department was achieved by switching the money away from the administrative service over a three-year period, after BBC1 was criticised, for failing in matching ITV's output in drama.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19">
        <a href="#cite_note-19">
         [19]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <i>
        EastEnders
       </i>
       was moved to a 19:30 slot, where it managed to soar to 20 million, helping the BBC1 audience share increase to nearly 50% for the first time since 1982.
      </p>
      <p>
       On 27 February 1985
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Doctor_Who" title="Doctor Who">
         Doctor Who
        </a>
       </i>
       was placed on an 18-month hiatus. The BBC originally planned to axe the series as they wished to spend its budgets on new programming for the channel, but was forced to back down from public pressure and Doctor Who returned in September 1986. At the time Michael Grade and Jonathan Powell were blamed for the decision (Grade was the target of death threats
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-transdiffusion.org_18-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-transdiffusion.org-18">
         [18]
        </a>
       </sup>
       ) but it was later revealed that the decision was taken due to the series running out of creative inspiration, making it impossible to find anyone (at the time) who knew what to do with the series.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20">
        <a href="#cite_note-20">
         [20]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21">
        <a href="#cite_note-21">
         [21]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       On 9 September 1985, the long-standing children's programming block was overhauled and rebranded as
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/CBBC" title="CBBC">
         Children's BBC
        </a>
       </i>
       , which gave it dedicated idents for the first time and had a live in-vision presenter unlike
       <a href="/wiki/CITV" title="CITV">
        Children's ITV
       </a>
       . Previously the BBC had broadcast children's programming using BBC1's team of regular duty announcers. The launch presenter for this block, and thus the first Children's BBC presenter of the current format, was
       <a href="/wiki/Phillip_Schofield" title="Phillip Schofield">
        Phillip Schofield
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       On 23 May 1986, long-running lunchtime magazine show
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Pebble_Mill_at_One" title="Pebble Mill at One">
         Pebble Mill at One
        </a>
       </i>
       was broadcast for the last time after 14 years on the air. Monday 27 October 1986 saw BBC1 launch its daytime television schedules.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22">
        <a href="#cite_note-22">
         [22]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In a statement, BBC Daytime head Roger Laughton said:
      </p>
      <table class="cquote pullquote" role="presentation" style="margin:auto; border-collapse: collapse; border: none; background-color: transparent; width: auto;">
       <tr>
        <td style="width: 20px; vertical-align: top; border: none; color: #B2B7F2; font-size: 40px; font-family: 'Times New Roman', Times, serif; font-weight: bold; line-height: .6em; text-align: left; padding: 10px 10px;">
         “
        </td>
        <td style="vertical-align: top; border: none; padding: 4px 10px;">
         It was the natural extension of the corporation's commitment to public service broadcasting, since half the population had access to television during the day mainly the retired, unemployed and housewives.
        </td>
        <td style="width: 20px; vertical-align: bottom; border: none; color: #B2B7F2; font-size: 40px; font-family: 'Times New Roman', Times, serif; font-weight: bold; line-height: .6em; text-align: right; padding: 10px 10px;">
         ”
        </td>
       </tr>
      </table>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="1990s">
        1990s
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=BBC_One&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: 1990s">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:BBC1-1991.svg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="680" data-file-width="915" height="163" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/BBC1-1991.svg/220px-BBC1-1991.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/BBC1-1991.svg/330px-BBC1-1991.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/BBC1-1991.svg/440px-BBC1-1991.svg.png 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:BBC1-1991.svg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Logo of BBC1 from 16 February 1991 to 4 October 1997
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Stereo audio transmissions, using the
       <a href="/wiki/NICAM" title="NICAM">
        NICAM
       </a>
       digital stereo sound format began on BBC1 at some point in autumn 1987, to coincide with the sale of the first consumer NICAM-enabled equipment, a year after BBC2, and were gradually phased in across BBC TV output, although it took until 31 August 1991 for the service to begin officially on both channels. During this time, both commercial analogue broadcasters,
       <a href="/wiki/ITV_(TV_network)" title="ITV (TV network)">
        ITV
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Channel_4" title="Channel 4">
        Channel 4
       </a>
       had officially begun
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stereo" title="Stereo">
        stereo
       </a>
       transmissions using the BBC-developed NICAM system. Widescreen programming was introduced on
       <a href="/wiki/Digital_television" title="Digital television">
        digital platforms
       </a>
       in 1998.
      </p>
      <p>
       For the first fifty years of its existence, with the exception of films and purchased programmes from the United States and elsewhere, almost all the channel's output was produced by the BBC's in-house production departments. This changed following the
       <a href="/wiki/Broadcasting_Act_1990" title="Broadcasting Act 1990">
        Broadcasting Act 1990
       </a>
       , which required that 25% of the BBC's television output be out-sourced to independent production companies.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23">
        <a href="#cite_note-23">
         [23]
        </a>
       </sup>
       By 2004 many popular BBC One shows were made for the channel by independents, but the in-house production departments continued to contribute heavily to the schedule.
      </p>
      <p>
       In March 1991, as part of the £63 million programme package for spring and summer line up on BBC1, it was announced an extra £20 million was to be spent on rejuvenating the channels drama and comedy output during peak times, which meant the channel would be in a healthy state once the new
       <a href="/wiki/ITV_(TV_network)" title="ITV (TV network)">
        Channel 3
       </a>
       licences were awarded.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24">
        <a href="#cite_note-24">
         [24]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       In December 1991
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Wogan" title="Wogan">
         Wogan
        </a>
       </i>
       was to be cancelled, due to falling ratings against a number of
       <a href="/wiki/ITV_(TV_network)" title="ITV (TV network)">
        ITV
       </a>
       shows, in which
       <i>
        Wogan
       </i>
       only managed six million viewers compared to double for
       <i>
        This Is Your Life
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        The Krypton Factor
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        The $64,000 Question
       </i>
       . Additionally an extra £40 million a year was spent on narrowing the gap on ITV's ratings lead, since a few months prior to this the channel had been criticised for its Autumn schedule, having tired formats, uninspiring scheduling of new programmes and poor scripts.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25">
        <a href="#cite_note-25">
         [25]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <i>
        Wogan
       </i>
       was replaced with
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Eldorado_(TV_series)" title="Eldorado (TV series)">
         Eldorado
        </a>
       </i>
       , in early July 1992, but this was itself cancelled a year later.
      </p>
      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/Alan_Yentob" title="Alan Yentob">
        Alan Yentob
       </a>
       launched the 1993 Autumn schedule calling it "My first try with a lot of help from my friends", with the channel still under criticism, following the start of new programming Alan introduced a year earlier and the amount of summer repeats. £175 million was spent on 80 hours of original drama produced, enchantment to the arts with an extended 26-week run for
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Omnibus_(UK_TV_series)" title="Omnibus (UK TV series)">
         Omnibus
        </a>
       </i>
       , and documentaries with
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/The_Downing_Street_Years" title="The Downing Street Years">
         The Downing Street Years
        </a>
       </i>
       , new wildlife series and an eight-month look at Sheffield's Children's hospital, while
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Goodnight_Sweetheart_(TV_series)" title="Goodnight Sweetheart (TV series)">
         Goodnight Sweetheart
        </a>
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Grace_%26_Favour" title="Grace &amp; Favour">
         Grace &amp; Favour
        </a>
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        The Danny Baker Show
       </i>
       were new comedy series.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26">
        <a href="#cite_note-26">
         [26]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <a href="/wiki/Lois_%26_Clark:_The_New_Adventures_of_Superman" title="Lois &amp; Clark: The New Adventures of Superman">
        The New Adventures of Superman
       </a>
       was brought in to give the Saturday night line up a bit of variety.
      </p>
      <p>
       Following the public disapproval of filling its schedule with 25% of repeats during the summer months in 1993, BBC1 agreed to broadcast an extra 110 hours worth of original programming over the same period during the summer in 1994, which included giving
       <i>
        EastEnders
       </i>
       an additional episode per week. Efficiency savings of £25 million were found which were redeployed on the new productions. The savings were seen as a vindication so for the producer choice, the controversial market-oriented drive introduced in April 1993.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27">
        <a href="#cite_note-27">
         [27]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28">
        <a href="#cite_note-28">
         [28]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       By March 1999, the channel admitted defeat in its ratings war with
       <a href="/wiki/ITV_(TV_network)" title="ITV (TV network)">
        ITV
       </a>
       , with its Spring line up with a stronger emphasis on serious factual programmes, educations and drama. This change in strategy came about after continuing complaints that the channel was appealing to the lowest common denominator to win viewers, which has left it chastened by the hoax guests on
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Vanessa_(UK_TV_series)" title="Vanessa (UK TV series)">
         Vanessa
        </a>
       </i>
       , over reliance on
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Docusoap" title="Docusoap">
        docusoaps
       </a>
       and the dropping of the vilified
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Noel%27s_House_Party" title="Noel's House Party">
         Noel's House Party
        </a>
       </i>
       . Alan Yentob said "The spring package is to remind people of what the BBC is here for, Range and ambition you won't find anywhere else at peak time". The changes help the channel distinguish itself from (as one BBC executive said) its downmarket rival and would not compete for viewers on ITV's terms."
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29">
        <a href="#cite_note-29">
         [29]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="2000s">
        2000s
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=BBC_One&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: 2000s">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/Lorraine_Heggessey" title="Lorraine Heggessey">
        Lorraine Heggessey
       </a>
       became Controller of BBC One, a post she took up on 1 November 2000.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-controller_30-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-controller-30">
         [30]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-swapper_31-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-swapper-31">
         [31]
        </a>
       </sup>
       She had previously been sounded out about the job in 1997 after
       <a href="/wiki/Michael_Jackson_(TV_executive)" title="Michael Jackson (TV executive)">
        Michael Jackson
       </a>
       's departure, but had turned down the opportunity as she felt she was then not yet experienced enough.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-offer_32-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-offer-32">
         [32]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       During Heggessey's five years in charge, BBC One's audience share fell by 19.9%, to 23%, although this was in the context of declining audience figures across all British television channels due to increased competition from multichannel
       <a href="/wiki/Digital_television" title="Digital television">
        digital television
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-first_33-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-first-33">
         [33]
        </a>
       </sup>
       However, in 2001 BBC One overtook its main rival
       <a href="/wiki/ITV_(TV_network)" title="ITV (TV network)">
        ITV
       </a>
       in terms of annual audience share for the first time since the rival channel had launched in 1955,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-itv1_34-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-itv1-34">
         [34]
        </a>
       </sup>
       although much of this was down to the success of the channel's
       <a href="/wiki/Daytime_television" title="Daytime television">
        daytime television
       </a>
       line-up, which had its own Controller:
       <a href="/wiki/Jane_Lush" title="Jane Lush">
        Jane Lush
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-itv1_34-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-itv1-34">
         [34]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       When Heggessey arrived at the channel in November 2000, she inherited two controversial schedule changes which had been implemented the previous month, at the behest of
       <a href="/wiki/Director-General_of_the_BBC" title="Director-General of the BBC">
        Director-General of the BBC
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/Greg_Dyke" title="Greg Dyke">
        Greg Dyke
       </a>
       ; the
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/BBC_Nine_O%27Clock_News" title="BBC Nine O'Clock News">
         Nine O'Clock News
        </a>
       </i>
       had been moved to the later time of 22:00 and
       <i>
        Panorama
       </i>
       moved from a Monday night prime time slot to a later slot on Sunday nights.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-panorama_35-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-panorama-35">
         [35]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The moving of
       <i>
        Panorama
       </i>
       attracted criticism that BBC One was sidelining serious programming in favour of more populist output.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-homer_36-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-homer-36">
         [36]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Heggessey publicly defended the decision, despite it not being hers, claiming that
       <i>
        Panorama'
       </i>
       s ratings would have "dwindled" in its previous slot.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-panorama_35-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-panorama-35">
         [35]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Heggessey and the BBC's Controller of Drama Commissioning,
       <a href="/wiki/Jane_Tranter" title="Jane Tranter">
        Jane Tranter
       </a>
       , took advantage of the weekday 21:00 slot opened up by the moving of the news to commission new popular drama output, such as the successful
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Waking_the_Dead_(TV_series)" title="Waking the Dead (TV series)">
         Waking the Dead
        </a>
       </i>
       (2000–2011) and
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Spooks_(TV_series)" title="Spooks (TV series)">
         Spooks
        </a>
       </i>
       (2002–2011).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-popular_37-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-popular-37">
         [37]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Celebrity dancing show
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Strictly_Come_Dancing" title="Strictly Come Dancing">
         Strictly Come Dancing
        </a>
       </i>
       (2004–present) was also a popular success on Saturday nights,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-popular_37-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-popular-37">
         [37]
        </a>
       </sup>
       although another Saturday night entertainment series,
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Fame_Academy" title="Fame Academy">
         Fame Academy
        </a>
       </i>
       , faced accusations of being too derivative of the output of commercial rivals, and during Heggessey's era the channel frequently came under attack for being too populist and not providing enough serious programming.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-attacks_38-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-attacks-38">
         [38]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       In 2002, Heggessey took the decision to abandon the traditional
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/BBC_television_idents#BBC_One" title="BBC television idents">
        "Globe" idents
       </a>
       the channel had used in a variety of forms for its between-programme
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Television_ident" title="Television ident">
        idents
       </a>
       since 1963. They were replaced by a new style of on-air identity for the channel, the
       <a href="/wiki/BBC_One_%27Rhythm_%26_Movement%27_idents" title="BBC One 'Rhythm &amp; Movement' idents">
        "Rhythm &amp; Movement" idents
       </a>
       . The new idents attracted criticism for going against the traditions of the channel
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-idents1_39-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-idents1-39">
         [39]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and pandering to
       <a href="/wiki/Political_correctness" title="Political correctness">
        political correctness
       </a>
       , as they featured activities performed by people of various ethnicities.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-idents2_40-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-idents2-40">
         [40]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The abandonment of a station clock, and perceived lack of a 'serious ident', also put the BBC in an embarrassing situation just one day into the new look with the death of the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Queen_Elizabeth_the_Queen_Mother" title="Queen Elizabeth the Queen Mother">
        Queen Mother
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       One of Heggessey's most notable decisions and last major success at the channel was the re-commissioning of the science-fiction drama series
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Doctor_Who" title="Doctor Who">
         Doctor Who
        </a>
       </i>
       , which had been a popular hit in previous decades but ceased production in 1989. Heggessey and Jane Tranter recommissioned the series in September 2003, after Heggessey had spent two years persuading the BBC's commercial arm,
       <a href="/wiki/BBC_Worldwide" title="BBC Worldwide">
        BBC Worldwide
       </a>
       , to abandon their attempts to make a
       <a href="/wiki/Feature_film" title="Feature film">
        feature film
       </a>
       version of the programme and allow it instead to return to BBC One.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-who1_41-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-who1-41">
         [41]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The new version of
       <i>
        Doctor Who
       </i>
       (2005–present) debuted on 26 March 2005 and became a critical and popular hit,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-who2_42-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-who2-42">
         [42]
        </a>
       </sup>
       with
       <a href="/wiki/Paul_Hoggart" title="Paul Hoggart">
        Paul Hoggart
       </a>
       of
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/The_Times" title="The Times">
         The Times
        </a>
       </i>
       newspaper describing the series as "a joyful, exuberant reinvention and a fine legacy from Ms Heggessey."
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-who3_43-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-who3-43">
         [43]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Heggessey did later concede in a 2005 interview with
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/The_Independent" title="The Independent">
         The Independent
        </a>
       </i>
       newspaper that
       <a href="/wiki/The_arts" title="The arts">
        arts
       </a>
       programming had suffered a cutback under her control of BBC One.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-swapper_31-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-swapper-31">
         [31]
        </a>
       </sup>
       However, she did respond to this omission following criticism from the
       <a href="/wiki/Board_of_Governors_of_the_BBC" title="Board of Governors of the BBC">
        Board of Governors of the BBC
       </a>
       by commissioning programmes such as the arts documentary series
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Imagine_(TV_series)" title="Imagine (TV series)">
         Imagine...
        </a>
       </i>
       (2003–present) and
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/A_Picture_of_Britain" title="A Picture of Britain">
         A Picture of Britain
        </a>
       </i>
       (2005).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-notdumb_44-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-notdumb-44">
         [44]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       On 14 February 2005 it was announced that Lorraine Heggessey was to leave the BBC to take up the post of Chief Executive at production company
       <a href="/wiki/Talkback_Thames" title="Talkback Thames">
        Talkback Thames
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-notdumb_44-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-notdumb-44">
         [44]
        </a>
       </sup>
       She left on 15 April. Five months after her departure, BBC One was named "Channel of the Year" at the
       <a href="/wiki/Edinburgh_International_Television_Festival" title="Edinburgh International Television Festival">
        Edinburgh Television Festival
       </a>
       , primarily on the strength of Heggessey commissions such as
       <i>
        Strictly Come Dancing
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        Doctor Who
       </i>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-year_45-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-year-45">
         [45]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Joining the channel as Controller in 2005,
       <a href="/wiki/Peter_Fincham" title="Peter Fincham">
        Peter Fincham
       </a>
       oversaw the commissioning of several successful BBC One programmes including
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Robin_Hood_(2006_TV_series)" title="Robin Hood (2006 TV series)">
         Robin Hood
        </a>
       </i>
       (2006–2009),
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Jane_Eyre_(2006_miniseries)" title="Jane Eyre (2006 miniseries)">
         Jane Eyre
        </a>
       </i>
       (2006) and
       <i>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/How_Do_You_Solve_a_Problem_Like_Maria%3F" title="How Do You Solve a Problem Like Maria?">
         How Do You Solve a Problem Like Maria?
        </a>
       </i>
       , which was followed by similar shows
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Any_Dream_Will_Do_(TV_series)" title="Any Dream Will Do (TV series)">
         Any Dream Will Do
        </a>
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/I%27d_Do_Anything_(BBC_TV_series)" title="I'd Do Anything (BBC TV series)">
         I'd Do Anything
        </a>
       </i>
       because of its success.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-snoddy_46-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-snoddy-46">
         [46]
        </a>
       </sup>
       His first full year in charge of the channel saw a year-on-year growth in the audience share, with a rise from 22.2% in August 2005 to 23.6% in August 2006.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-audience_47-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-audience-47">
         [47]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Fincham also directly initiated the creation of both
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/The_One_Show" title="The One Show">
         The One Show
        </a>
       </i>
       (2006–present), an early evening, current-affairs and lifestyle magazine programme, which now runs all but two weeks of the year, and
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Davina_(talk_show)" title="Davina (talk show)">
         Davina
        </a>
       </i>
       (2006), a prime time
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chat_show" title="Chat show">
        chat show
       </a>
       , the latter hosted by
       <a href="/wiki/Davina_McCall" title="Davina McCall">
        Davina McCall
       </a>
       , who presented
       <i>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Big_Brother_(UK)" title="Big Brother (UK)">
         Big Brother
        </a>
       </i>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-oneshow_48-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-oneshow-48">
         [48]
        </a>
       </sup>
       However,
       <i>
        Davina
       </i>
       was a critical and ratings disaster,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-davina1_49-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-davina1-49">
         [49]
        </a>
       </sup>
       which Fincham subsequently admitted was personally his fault, although he defended the strategy of experimenting with the BBC One schedule. This he continued in January 2007, when he moved the
       <a href="/wiki/Current_affairs_(news_format)" title="Current affairs (news format)">
        current affairs
       </a>
       series
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Panorama_(TV_series)" title="Panorama (TV series)">
         Panorama
        </a>
       </i>
       from its Sunday night slot back to the prime time Monday evening slot from which it had been removed in 2000, most likely in response to a demand from the
       <a href="/wiki/Board_of_Governors_of_the_BBC" title="Board of Governors of the BBC">
        Board of Governors of the BBC
       </a>
       for the channel to show more current affairs programming in prime time.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50">
        <a href="#cite_note-50">
         [50]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:BBC_One_2002.png">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="147" data-file-width="200" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/1/1a/BBC_One_2002.png" width="200"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:BBC_One_2002.png" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Logo of BBC One from 29 March 2002 to 7 October 2006
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Fincham's judgement was again called into question, this time by
       <i>
        The Telegraph
       </i>
       , for his decision to spend £1.2 million replacing the channel's
       <a href="/wiki/BBC_One_%27Rhythm_%26_Movement%27_idents" title="BBC One 'Rhythm &amp; Movement' idents">
        'Rhythm and Movement' idents
       </a>
       , which had been introduced by his predecessor
       <a href="/wiki/Lorraine_Heggessey" title="Lorraine Heggessey">
        Lorraine Heggessey
       </a>
       several years earlier, with the
       <a href="/wiki/BBC_One_%27Circle%27_idents" title="BBC One 'Circle' idents">
        'Circle' idents
       </a>
       , a set of eight ten-second films, some of which were shot abroad in locations such as Mexico and Croatia.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-idents_51-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-idents-51">
         [51]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Fincham later found himself having to publicly defend the £18 million salary that the BBC paid
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jonathan_Ross_(television_presenter)" title="Jonathan Ross (television presenter)">
        Jonathan Ross
       </a>
       in 2006, although Ross's BBC One work—primarily consisting of
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Friday_Night_with_Jonathan_Ross" title="Friday Night with Jonathan Ross">
         Friday Night with Jonathan Ross
        </a>
       </i>
       —formed only part of his overall BBC commitment.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ross_52-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-ross-52">
         [52]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The channel was named
       <i>
        Channel of the Year
       </i>
       at the 2007 Broadcast Awards.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53">
        <a href="#cite_note-53">
         [53]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="The_One_to_Watch_campaign">
        The One to Watch campaign
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=BBC_One&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: The One to Watch campaign">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       Following its rebrand in March 2002, BBC One launched
       <i>
        The One to Watch
       </i>
       campaign, during which animated blocks created the word "The" and moved into the
       <a href="/wiki/BBC" title="BBC">
        BBC
       </a>
       logo. Each new campaign incorporating the theme retained the same animated sequence.
      </p>
      <p>
       In May 2007, Fincham took the decision to drop
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Neighbours" title="Neighbours">
         Neighbours
        </a>
       </i>
       , an Australian soap opera, from BBC One after 21 years on the channel, when its producers significantly raised the price they wanted the BBC to pay for it in a bidding war.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54">
        <a href="#cite_note-54">
         [54]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Fincham commented that it was 'a big loss', but that BBC One would not pay 'the best part of £300 million'.
       <i>
        Neighbours
       </i>
       left the channel in spring 2008 to move to
       <a href="/wiki/Channel_5_(UK)" title="Channel 5 (UK)">
        Channel 5
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NeighMove_55-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-NeighMove-55">
         [55]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/The_Weakest_Link_(UK_game_show)" title="The Weakest Link (UK game show)">
         The Weakest Link
        </a>
       </i>
       was moved from BBC Two to fill the gap, with the afternoon
       <a href="/wiki/CBBC" title="CBBC">
        CBBC
       </a>
       slot moving 20 minutes earlier.
      </p>
      <p>
       There was further controversy in July 2007 when Fincham was accused of misleading BBC One viewers. The incident involved a clip from forthcoming documentary
       <i>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/A_Year_with_the_Queen" title="A Year with the Queen">
         A Year with the Queen
        </a>
       </i>
       which was shown to journalists during a press conference. It apparently showed
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Elizabeth_II_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Elizabeth II of the United Kingdom">
        the Queen
       </a>
       storming out of a session with American photographer
       <a href="/wiki/Annie_Leibovitz" title="Annie Leibovitz">
        Annie Leibovitz
       </a>
       over a disagreement about what she should wear, but the BBC subsequently admitted that the scenes used in the trailer had been edited out of their correct order, meaning that a false impression was given.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56">
        <a href="#cite_note-56">
         [56]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Fincham admitted the error, but rejected calls that he should resign from his position as a result.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57">
        <a href="#cite_note-57">
         [57]
        </a>
       </sup>
       His future was deemed uncertain following critical comments from
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Michael_Lyons_(UK_politician)" title="Michael Lyons (UK politician)">
        Sir Michael Lyons
       </a>
       , Chairman of the
       <a href="/wiki/BBC_Trust" title="BBC Trust">
        BBC Trust
       </a>
       and he resigned on 5 October 2007.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58">
        <a href="#cite_note-58">
         [58]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       In 2009, a report published by the
       <a href="/wiki/BBC_Trust" title="BBC Trust">
        BBC Trust
       </a>
       found said scheduling changes had led to a decrease in viewers.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59">
        <a href="#cite_note-59">
         [59]
        </a>
       </sup>
       This was especially noticeable for
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Blue_Peter" title="Blue Peter">
         Blue Peter
        </a>
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Newsround" title="Newsround">
         Newsround
        </a>
       </i>
       , two of CBBC's flagship programmes;
       <i>
        Blue Peter
       </i>
       which recorded its lowest viewing numbers since it started in 1958, and
       <i>
        Newsround
       </i>
       with fewer than 100,000 viewers compared to 225,000 in 2007.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60">
        <a href="#cite_note-60">
         [60]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61">
        <a href="#cite_note-61">
         [61]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="2010s">
        2010s
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=BBC_One&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: 2010s">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       As part of the Delivering Quality First proposals submitted by the BBC in October 2011 and approved by the
       <a href="/wiki/BBC_Trust" title="BBC Trust">
        BBC Trust
       </a>
       in May 2012, all children's programming on BBC One and Two would be moved permanently to the CBBC and CBeebies channels following the digital switchover.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62">
        <a href="#cite_note-62">
         [62]
        </a>
       </sup>
       It was found that the majority of child viewers watched the programmes on these channels already and that only 7% of these children watched CBBC programmes on BBC One and Two only, it was made clear "Children's programmes are absolutely fundamental to the BBC and that is why we have protected investment in them in the light of cuts elsewhere."
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DQF_conclusions_63-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-DQF_conclusions-63">
         [63]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Children's programming on BBC One ended on 21 December 2012.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64">
        <a href="#cite_note-64">
         [64]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The move was criticised by
       <i>
        Teletubbies
       </i>
       co-creator
       <a href="/wiki/Anne_Wood" title="Anne Wood">
        Anne Wood
       </a>
       , who described the changes as "ghettoising children's programmes" and believe it was merely a cost-cutting measure. Wood said "On the one hand it is inevitable. But it is dismissive of children. There is a certain amount of overlooking of the fact that children's programmes do get a wider audience than people are aware of ... I have frequently had letters from older people who have enjoyed my programmes as much as children do. A lot of the reason older people like to watch children's programming is because it is life-enhancing." Head of BBC Children's, Joe Godwin said: "Our young viewers are our priority and the vast majority of children in the UK already tune in to CBeebies and CBBC to find their favourite BBC children's programmes. Far from being a 'cynical' move, we're just following where our audience has already gone."
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65">
        <a href="#cite_note-65">
         [65]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       As part of the review in 2012 other changes were brought in, including:
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        BBC One is reducing the minimum hours of arts and music from 45 to 40, achieved through cutting episodes of shows, in particular
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Film_2013" title="Film 2013">
         Film 2013
        </a>
        .
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66">
         <a href="#cite_note-66">
          [66]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        BBC One and Two will "largely be protected from making significant cuts".
       </li>
       <li>
        Repeats on BBC One will increase, but remain under 10% of all output (the current rate is 8.4%
        <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
         [
         <i>
          <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
           <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2017)">
            citation needed
           </span>
          </a>
         </i>
         ]
        </sup>
        ).
       </li>
       <li>
        Expenditure on sports rights will be cut by 15%. This had largely been achieved already by sharing rights to Formula 1 coverage from 2012 (it was later dropped entirely from 2016).
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67">
         <a href="#cite_note-67">
          [67]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
      </ul>
      <p>
       In 2012, the BBC out-bid ITV for the rights to
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/The_Voice_UK" title="The Voice UK">
         The Voice UK
        </a>
       </i>
       , which had already proved to be popular in other countries. The BBC paid £22 million for the rights to broadcast the show in the UK for two years.
       <i>
        The Voice UK
       </i>
       achieved good ratings for the BBC but ratings dropped towards the end of the first series and the second series. In 2013,
       <i>
        The Voice
       </i>
       was rescheduled to avoid a clash, and as a result, ratings have improved. In November 2015, it was announced that
       <i>
        The Voice
       </i>
       would be moving to ITV from 2017.
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Other_services">
        Other services
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=BBC_One&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Other services">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <h3>
       <span id="BBC_One_.2B1">
       </span>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="BBC_One_+1">
        BBC One +1
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=BBC_One&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: BBC One +1">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       On 8 October 2013, the BBC announced plans to launch a one-hour timeshift of the channel, named BBC One +1.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68">
        <a href="#cite_note-68">
         [68]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The channel would have replaced
       <a href="/wiki/BBC_Three" title="BBC Three">
        BBC Three
       </a>
       in 2016. However, On 30 June 2015, the BBC Trust rejected the plans for a BBC One +1 channel as they stated that it would be at the expense of commercial rivals.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69">
        <a href="#cite_note-69">
         [69]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="BBC_One_HD">
        BBC One HD
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=BBC_One&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: BBC One HD">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:BBC_One_HD.svg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="366" data-file-width="948" height="85" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/BBC_One_HD.svg/220px-BBC_One_HD.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/BBC_One_HD.svg/330px-BBC_One_HD.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/BBC_One_HD.svg/440px-BBC_One_HD.svg.png 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:BBC_One_HD.svg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         BBC One HD logo
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       <b>
        BBC One HD
       </b>
       , a
       <a href="/wiki/Simulcast" title="Simulcast">
        simulcast
       </a>
       of BBC One in
       <a href="/wiki/720p" title="720p">
        720p
       </a>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70">
        <a href="#cite_note-70">
         [70]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <a href="/wiki/High-definition_television" title="High-definition television">
        high-definition
       </a>
       (HD), launched on 3 November 2010 at 19:00 with The One Show.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71">
        <a href="#cite_note-71">
         [71]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The channel simulcasts a network version of BBC One in High Definition, with HD versions of programmes including
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Doctor_Who" title="Doctor Who">
         Doctor Who
        </a>
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Holby_City" title="Holby City">
         Holby City
        </a>
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/The_One_Show" title="The One Show">
         The One Show
        </a>
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Strictly_Come_Dancing" title="Strictly Come Dancing">
         Strictly Come Dancing
        </a>
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/The_Apprentice_(UK_TV_series)" title="The Apprentice (UK TV series)">
         The Apprentice
        </a>
       </i>
       .
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/EastEnders" title="EastEnders">
         EastEnders
        </a>
       </i>
       was also made available in HD from Christmas Day 2010. All programmes still made in standard-definition were upscaled on the channel, with the intention that by 2012 the vast majority of the channel's output would be in high-definition. On 30 May 2012, the satellite and terrestrial resolution was increased to
       <a href="/wiki/1080p" title="1080p">
        full HD
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:No_original_research" title="Wikipedia:No original research">
          <span title="The material near this tag possibly contains original research. (June 2014)">
           original research?
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       BBC One HD at launch did not offer regional variations, and therefore the channel could not broadcast during regional programming slots, most noticeably the local news programmes. The BBC Trust admitted that this was due to technical and financial constraints,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72">
        <a href="#cite_note-72">
         [72]
        </a>
       </sup>
       but the BBC announced on 6 June 2011 that the national variations of BBC One Northern Ireland, BBC One Scotland and BBC One Wales, would become available from 2012.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73">
        <a href="#cite_note-73">
         [73]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74">
        <a href="#cite_note-74">
         [74]
        </a>
       </sup>
       On 24 October 2012, Northern Ireland received the first variation. A Scottish variation launched on 14 January 2013, followed by a Welsh variation on 29 January 2013.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75">
        <a href="#cite_note-75">
         [75]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Unlike BBC One HD, which is capable of broadcasting audio content in full 5.1 DTS, BBC One Wales HD and BBC One Northern Ireland HD are both currently only broadcasting audio in PCM stereo, even when programming is otherwise identical to that of BBC One HD. On 16 July 2013, the BBC indicated that it also wants to launch regional variants of BBC One HD across England, however this would require the approval of the BBC Trust, with a proposal due to be presented within six months.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76">
        <a href="#cite_note-76">
         [76]
        </a>
       </sup>
       On 18 November 2013, the Northern Irish regional variant of BBC One HD was swapped with the SD channel on Sky's EPG for HD subscribers. This was followed by the Welsh and Scottish variants on 10 December.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77">
        <a href="#cite_note-77">
         [77]
        </a>
       </sup>
       On 24 March 2014, BBC One Scotland, Wales and Northern Ireland HD launched on Freesat, Sky and Virgin Media outside the regions they were originally seen in.
      </p>
      <p>
       On 31 March 2016, BBC One HD in
       <a href="/wiki/England" title="England">
        England
       </a>
       moved from channel 141 on the
       <a href="/wiki/Sky_UK" title="Sky UK">
        Sky
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/Electronic_program_guide" title="Electronic program guide">
        electronic programme guide
       </a>
       to channel 115, a position vacated by
       <a href="/wiki/BBC_Three" title="BBC Three">
        BBC Three
       </a>
       , which had been switched to internet-only six weeks earlier. Changes in
       <a href="/wiki/Scotland" title="Scotland">
        Scotland
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Wales" title="Wales">
        Wales
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Northern_Ireland" title="Northern Ireland">
        Northern Ireland
       </a>
       were also scheduled but delayed for 'technical reasons'.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78">
        <a href="#cite_note-78">
         [78]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Contemporary_programming">
        Contemporary programming
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=BBC_One&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Contemporary programming">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <table class="plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Refimprove" role="presentation">
       <tr>
        <td class="mbox-image">
         <div style="width:52px">
          <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg">
           <img alt="" data-file-height="399" data-file-width="512" height="39" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/50px-Question_book-new.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/75px-Question_book-new.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/100px-Question_book-new.svg.png 2x" width="50"/>
          </a>
         </div>
        </td>
        <td class="mbox-text">
         <div class="mbox-text-span">

          <b>
           needs additional citations for
           <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">

           </a>
          </b>

           </i>

      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/List_of_television_programmes_broadcast_by_the_BBC" title="List of television programmes broadcast by the BBC">
        List of television programmes broadcast by the BBC
       </a>
      </div>
      <blockquote class="templatequote">
       <p>
        BBC One's remit is to be the BBC's most popular mixed-genre television service across the UK, offering a wide range of high quality programmes. It should be the BBC's primary outlet for major UK and international events and it should reflect the whole of the UK in its output. A very high proportion of its programmes should be original productions.
       </p>
       <div class="templatequotecite">
        —
        <cite>
         <b>
          BBC One remit
         </b>
         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79">
          <a href="#cite_note-79">
           [79]
          </a>
         </sup>
        </cite>
       </div>
      </blockquote>
      <p>
       Excluding sporting events and news coverage, the top five most watched programmes at their peak viewing points (according to
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/BARB" title="BARB">
        BARB
       </a>
       ) were:
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80">
        <a href="#cite_note-80">
         [80]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>

         <a href="/wiki/Mark_Fowler" title="Mark Fowler">

         <a href="/wiki/Michelle_Fowler" title="Michelle Fowler">

         about his illness.
        </td>

         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bfi.org.uk_84-0">

         Michelle tells Den that she is pregnant.
        </td>
        <td>
         24.15
         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85">
          <a href="#cite_note-85">
           [85]
          </a>
         </sup>
        </td>
        <td>
         <span class="sortkey" style="display:none;speak:none">
